Mr Mogotsi: You see, this is now, if you check it correctly, it goes with almost the date of the recording. And that's why he said, if the information he gives is worth it, General Khan should help him. But I never appreciated it, because I couldn't get hold of him. I didn't know him.
Adv Mokhoaetsi: But do you see how even more complicated it gets with your explanation? Because your explanation states that, was there really then an issue? Mr Mogotsi: No, I wanted to know-
Adv Mokhoaetsi: Do you understand how it looks?
Mr Mogotsi: But the issue is, I wanted to know if somehow Mr Matlala sent this man to say all these things. To me, it was like he kept on talking about the bag, as to whether he wanted money. Mr Mogotsi: This is the advert which went to the media.

Mr M Ntuli (ANC): Chair, can I just indicate to you that there is a commitment which I can't postpone. I doubt I'm going to take even more than 15 minutes. I think the first question I wanted to raise, Mr Mogotsi, the investigations that you were conducting about Lieutenant-General Mkwanazi, His Majesty the Zulu King Misuzuli kaZwelithini, related to them being captured or working with the CIA. In your information, they work with them to what end? What is the objective? Why do they work with them? And maybe to explain the extent to which, in terms of your findings, their work for the CIA is succeeding or failing? And if it is failing, what are the reasons? If it is succeeding, what should be done to stop it?
Mr Mogotsi: As I have alluded, the case which was opened in Midrand by the 8th and the Prince Zulu, in their statement, the suspicions emanate from what they have written insofar as the Richards Bay Terminal was concerned. And they thought that now, in that statement, the coal may not be going to, because that coal was benefiting Israel so much. But the investigation was as a result of the statement that was written in the case that was opened in Midrand.
Mr Ntuli: So they were intervening in the work that they were engaged in, in terms of your information. You are saying that the coal that is shipped out of Richards Bay, most of it is intended for Israel. Is that what you are saying?
Mr Mogotsi: That’s correct.
Mr Ntuli: So when they were intervening, His Majesty and the Lieutenant-General Mkhwanazi, they were intervening in order to do what? In order to make sure that the coal continues to be sent in big volumes to Israel, or intervene against the people who were intending to block that coal from going to Israel? Mr Mogotsi: What I have raised earlier was that even so, the same Prince that were in engagement, the intention from that discussion was that the plan was to contract these Chinese to also utilise the terminal as opposed to those that are transporting to Israel. And the conflict started there, and the case was precisely opened because, according to them, the PKTT members and Lieutenant-General Mkhwanazi were sending a lot of police to harass them, to threaten them, and in those altercations, they would keep making reference to the coal. Now the case was opened two or three times in KZN, and the matters could not be investigated. Matter’s gone anywhere. That's how they got in touch through my handler to come and open a case in Gauteng.
Mr Ntuli: So what is the role of the king in that case? Because in your statement, you are making references to the fact that we know there is litigation, a legal dispute relating to the Zulu kingdom. Now you are making references to the fact that the side of the Zulu family, if we were to put it that way, which is picking up Prince Smakade to become the king, got abused and harassed by PKTT Members, and you are saying that harassment is a result of the loyalty of His Majesty King Misuzulu kaZwelithini to the Israelis. Mr Mogotsi: Because these other ones, the intention was to do the contract with the Chinese.
Mr Ntuli: Which one? The Smakade?
Mr Mogotsi: The Smakade. They were going to be part of the contract with the Chinese, and the power of the KZN provincial police was influenced to harass them from making that decision. Now, if you check, I remember he was asking this question as to whether you have the authority to sign this contract, because there’s His Majesty who could possibly sign on behalf of the kingdom. And he said that particular portion, in terms of whatever, belongs as part of his rights.
Mr Ntuli: You know, there is a line of discussion that Adv Mokhoaetsi was following with you and all of us; many of us were keenly interested in your answers, but it was difficult, really, to get a proper explanation. So let me try to rephrase part of that question, and I will give you an example. For instance, in your statement, on paragraph 103, you are making references to your assisting Mr Matlala to open a case with SAPS in Soweto, but we also know, not from that paragraph, that Mr Matlala made certain favours to you in terms of money, booking certain people. Now, what is not coming out very clearly, which is what she was asking you to clarify, is why are you so cosy, very close, to the extent that you acquire favours from the person that you are actually and effectively investigating, because he is part of a particular criminal network in our country? Mr Mogotsi: First, I must emphasise, I do not believe Mr ‘Cat’ Matlala is a member of this Big Five. I don't believe it. And that's what happened. And it may be wrong, at the layman's thinking, to say this man, even though he’s being investigated, he offered, he actually insisted he wanted to buy a table at that time, and he couldn't buy the table. And secondly, even the handler said he must not be involved with the minister, he must not come to the minister, and we stood by assisting with the travelling and stuff, R148 000.
Mr Ntuli: You are also saying in paragraph 104 that Mr Matlala told you that General Masemola as well as General Cele wanted to oust Minister Mchunu. Did he explain for what purpose they were engaged in such a-
Mr Mogotsi: He did not explain, but I believe that before this Committee, he did mention that former Minister Bheki Cele wanted a way to arrest Mr Senzo Mchunu. He did speak, and that's what he told me. And he never gave information as to how they were going to do that.
Mr Ntuli: Why did he not ask? Because I mean, somebody comes to you and shares information as negative as that one. Why would you decide to ignore making a follow-up question as to what purpose?
Mr Mogotsi: It's not to ignore as such. One would think that Mr Cele, now that he’s no longer here, that's the only reason he wants to remove Minister-
Mr Ntuli: No, that cannot be a rational explanation because ministers come and go. We have no evidence that when ministers are removed, or their term of office has ended, all of them then queue up to plot some agenda to oust their successors. So we cannot accept as a rational conclusion on your part that I didn't ask this question because I knew that now that he is not coming back, he may be having vengeance towards Mr Mchunu. Mr Cele was not removed by Mchunu. His term of office came to an end, and he did not come back to Parliament. You are saying that as well in your own statement. And then somebody comes to you and says there is a plot that is going on, and people who are engineering the plot are the chief of police in the country, working with the former minister. Why would a responsible South African like you simply not ask a question, but to what extent? Because these things might have serious security implications for our country, for you, for your family and everybody else. So why can't you ask a question: for what purpose?
Mr Mogotsi: The difference is, as human beings, what you think I'll never think. If a person gives me this information, obviously, I'll just let my handler know that there is this plot, because he further spoke about making sure that General Sibiya is out of the service. If I informed my handler, that's where I stopped. But I did not ask him, and I took it very seriously to an extent that one would say, we'll see how that happens.
Mr Ntuli: But Mr Mogotsi, you are, from your own statement, and I'm going to come to that issue later just to deal with it a bit, you have informed us that you were trained as part of the self-defence unit, which was almost something like a successor following the disbandment of Umkhonto WeSizwe. And surely your level of consciousness about matters of security cannot be equivalent to mine, who is a pure civilian. Somebody comes to you and says I have information that the chief of police in this country is engaged in an agenda or a plot to oust a sitting minister of police. How does it become normal that you don't ask, where do you get that information, and for what reason do you think they are engaged in that plot?
Mr Mogotsi: If he says he heard it from Mr Cele, as I said, I did tell my handler. Obviously, he has informed the relevant people about handling that. Information as it comes, my job is to make sure it reaches where it must go. And it's not that I was meeting Mr Matlala for the first or the last time. I was happy that he came, and he did say the former minister said he must give me money so that they can arrest Mr Senzo Mchunu. He said that. And nobody in the Committee asked him the same questions that you are asking. At least, the person who told me was telling the same Committee, nobody was asking, but to what extent and why would he want to do that? Because at the time, when you talk to somebody, it's not everything that person will think that will automatically come to me. I may have a different approach to this as opposed to how another Member would expect me to do.
Mr Ntuli: We can deal with what questions we pose to Mr Matlala, and whether, in your own opinion, those questions were insufficient, we can deal with that maybe much later. But the issue is about you, because when the interaction happened between you and Mr Matlala, you had no knowledge that at some point these matters may be ventilated before the ad hoc Committee. And I am asking you about your frame of mind at that time. As a trained person in security, how should South Africans see you in the context of that? Remember, you are not an ordinary civilian who has had no exposure to training, but we are being told by somebody, ‘I have information that the former minister is engaged in an agenda to oust the sitting minister and is working in cahoots with the national commissioner of police’, and then you just go away and say I'm going to tell my handler and that's all. What did your handler say when you told him or her?
Mr Mogotsi: He’ll handle it, and he’ll report it to the relevant-
Mr Ntuli: So he didn't care about the details?
Mr Mogotsi: No, it's not that he didn't care. There are so many things that happened after that. That is why even when I came to Cape Town, I made sure that the handler was among the list of people who came with me to Cape Town during this thing of Mr Matlala, because we thought he was also coming, and he didn't come.
Mr Ntuli: One of the observations I've made, Mr Mogotsi, arising from your statement and your interaction here with the evidence leaders, is that the nature of your work then, or today, I don't know whether you are no longer part of that, say, what is Crime Intelligence operation, whatever the title that you have, but my sense is that the nature of the work you are engaged on relies a lot, that work relies a lot on misleading, telling lies, and also sometimes depends on what I call subtifod (spelling unconfirmed). Will I be wrong when I say, because you interact with Mr Matlala in a particular way, you conceal certain information from him, you go and interact with somebody else, Lieutenant-General Mkhwanazi, you go to other people, but the nature of your work means that you must be somebody who, in your conduct, has the capacity to tell lies because you don't want people to know your actual motives. Am I wrong or right if I'm saying that?
Mr Mogotsi: You are correct, provided you report to the handler. And I'll give an example: you cannot take papers and apply for a loan fraudulently and say I was on duty. You cannot drive a police car, marked, because the police officer is probably sleeping, or you have access because you say you are an agent. This legend-building applies properly and must be reported. If there is a CIT to take place on Friday, you talk to a handler, you activate the police, there will be someone involved, and this person will call himself Jabu. He will say he knows where to borrow the firearms. I come again to tell my handler: they say they have to rent firearms; we need money to rent firearms.
Mr Ntuli: But in summary, you are agreeing with me that the nature of your work engages with the business of telling lies.
Mr Mogotsi: But it's called creating a false life.
Mr Ntuli: Should we therefore not be surprised in future as a Committee if we discover that in your interaction with us you also have falsified certain things? Should we depart from this sitting understanding that in future we may discover, because the people you have been engaging with and falsifying information, only happen to discover that situation through the Madlanga Commission, and I'm sure it's validated by your appearance before us. So I'm saying, what could be the reasons for us not to believe that tomorrow, which may not be just the day after today but tomorrow in the sense of the future, we are likely to discover that some of the assertions you have shared with us are not factually correct and they are not true, and that's part of the nature of your work?
Mr Mogotsi: It cannot happen like that. This is not an operation. This is now an appearance where you take an oath, and the intention is to help the Committee with the truth, with the information, to arrive at a certain conclusion. And in case there is something, I'll give an example: I don't know where this question is coming from. I was talking to the evidence leader, showing her a certain name, saying, you know this name, how this person died, I'm scared to show this name. But I ended up giving the name to her because she'll have to come either to the Chair or to you to say, this we're not going to talk about; rather, we hide it. I cannot lie on this platform. It's not an operation. This is a hearing.
Mr Ntuli: Now you are also making reference to yourself, as I think somewhere you are saying, in addition to these activities of the self-defence unit, you are also associated with, or a member of, the ANC. I want to assume that, knowing, I'm quite certain you know that I'm also a member of the ANC, I want to assume that you are familiar with the fact that part of what constitutes the value system of the ANC is being honest, being principled, and to be beyond reproach in your private and public life, which means not to do the things that you are doing in your life, which is to tell lies as part of your work. Why do you think that, because when you square the circle with somebody whose survival depends on falsifications and lies to a larger extent, and what I'm saying briefly are values of the organisation you claim to belong to, how do we put a square to that and say this is possible?
Mr Mogotsi: Everyone, a person goes to work, and the ANC Constitution doesn't say people can kill, people can do whatever. If a soldier goes to work, he gets there, he fights, he kills people, it doesn't mean he's being politically incorrect when he's doing his work. And I'm saying you can't lie to achieve a false life, to achieve something that is not tangible. It must be something that will help the country. I’ll give an example about the recording, the same recording. Here comes a guy, like I've been questioned, did you verify? What if he lied? And I say, it's a police officer. He says it's a plot to fire this general from work, but for me to help you, he's talking to certain people, ‘I want the promotion or something. Now, the same police officer who knows my job, they bring me in because a police officer can't lie and say I'm working in the minister's office. He cannot write that affidavit, but I, slowly, my handler knows, I wrote that; we recorded the guy; the guy is being charged. Now, this is exactly what happened in Madlanga. You know why? It is the first time someone comes in public and speaks like I do, that this is what I'm doing. You know, today here in Cape Town, I saw one colleague of mine doing the same thing.
Mr Ntuli: Lying?
Mr Mogotsi: Not lying. I know his work, but I will see him with a different person. I will see him, like, I cannot tell this person, hey, this man is like me, but it must be within the confines of this principle that you are giving.
Mr Ntuli: But would you agree with me, Mr Mogotsi, that in the light of what we now know about you, and we're still going to know, as my colleagues are engaging with you, it's quite clear that those who see themselves as genuine members of the party you claim to belong to would not want to be associated with a character like that one. I know you may want to say, well, there are other people in the ANC who are doing wrong things, other people who are this, they themselves, those ones, those ones that you may be thinking of now as you are about to answer my question, equally would not be belonging to what is meant to be a genuine membership of the ANC.
Mr Mogotsi: And that's why I don't stand, I don't occupy any position, I'm just a member of the ANC. And I even said, being a Methodist, being fired from church cannot stop you from praying, and constitutionally I will not do anything in my branch or in the ANC that results in being associated with-
Mr Ntuli: I thought you were saying you would not do anything that has to do with your responsibilities as a member because they don't deserve somebody like you.
Mr Mogotsi: In my branch, maybe you saw it when the PKTT goes there, how much I go out for my sub-region and my province. And you know, I don't know, this is so difficult, that's why I asked if possible, if through the Chairperson, one can just bring three or four projects or missions that I have done, and you check, you will see a different name and the outcome, conviction and sentence, as to what you must call me. It's unfortunate, but that is the life I'm in now.

Mr Mogotsi: But the handler has that authority to open a file number. Let me show you. If a case is opened here in Cape Town and a person in KZN has a case number, he will be able to pass it to the computer and see that a case has been opened here. So if you investigate Brown, and he is perhaps also a police officer, you will have a manual file. This file number operates almost like a case number. That is why, with a file number, you can even do a 205 to locate if two people were together, and the cell phones and LMS. You can follow someone on the phone with a file number. And that file number, once there is tangible or what you call prima facie evidence, is when it can be converted into a case number and be given to the police, and then they can run with it.

Mr Malema: Mr Nomvalo, you are leaving an important point. We do not just investigate people in Crime Intelligence. We do not just wake up in the morning and say we are investigating this person. What is the process?
Mr Mogotsi: In terms of? I do not understand. Process in terms of, depending on the crime.
Mr Malema: Yes. Someone comes and gives you information about me, and then you take the information to the handler. Now I must be investigated formally. What is the procedure?
Mr Mogotsi: This will give me an opportunity, really. Let us say the case is related to car theft, to cars or firearms. If the police station is Alexandra and the handler is in Soweto, but the information comes through an informant or whoever, and the head is in Soweto, the first thing he is going to do is to identify the police station or the area of jurisdiction. Then he will tell the informant, or whoever brings the information, what it is that you are talking about. You say, no, it depends. What is it that must be investigated? I wish you had given me a clue that so-and-so is stealing cars. The first thing he may ask is, do you have his cell phone numbers? Where did they steal their cars? As soon as you get your cell phone numbers, the handler must talk to whoever he has to talk to. There are two options. If you have already stolen that car, it means the case is open. But if you are plotting to steal the car, the handler must activate the unit, let us say the motor vehicle theft unit, and say there is a plot to steal a car on Friday, and the police will go and wait if you are going to commit a crime.
But let us talk in the case of SASSA cards. Someone is stealing cards or opening an account and withdrawing money from SASSA. You see, that one requires investigation. You cannot tell the police to stand by to arrest the person. This way, Crime Intelligence will have a file number. They will not register a case. With a file number, they will identify all the instruments that you are using to commit that crime. If a cell phone is involved, they will either look for the X70, like for your phone to be listened to.
Mr Malema: No, I hear you. We do not have too much time. So your answer is that Crime Intelligence does not open cases.
Mr Mogotsi: Crime Intelligence can have a file. The case and the docket, Crime Intelligence, no one can find them in the docket. The docket is handled by the detective and the police uniform, including the DPCI. They have dockets. Crime Intelligence will sit with the detective and the DPCI, gather the information and give it to them. But before the case is opened, you can have a file number so that they can go to court and apply to listen to your phone, apply to follow you and all those things through the file number, which must be approved by the divisional or sectional head.

Everyone gets so personal. South Africans must see he is a liar, and that is exactly what you are doing. Because you are not going to achieve what you are here for. Why am I saying that? Acting Chairperson: Let us do it like this, Mr Shongwe-

Then somebody comes here, and you feel that South Africans must see. I think this is what happens when you go home, when you prepare. Everyone prepares-
Mr Shongwe: Sorry to disturb you. Just for the sake of time, I only have 10 minutes or 15 minutes. I am not like them. I just want clarity. I want you to understand my question. You say that the case was dropped because there was no witness. What happened to the witness?

Mr Mogotsi: He was sick.
Mr Shongwe: Okay, let us go back. This is my question. Mr Mogotsi: I agree.

Mr Mogotsi: You do not get the point.

That is when, in 1996, they started integrating the majority of them into the statutory force.
Mr Sauls: That is actually incorrect. The first phase started in 1995.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, I am talking about 1996.
Mr Sauls: 1996 is not the start of it. Now I asked you, is that when it started? You said yes. So it started in 1995. It was a process that carried on until 1998. Then they were fully integrated. Now, how old were you in 1996?
Mr Mogotsi: I was 17, yes.
Mr Sauls: So then, at 17, going on 18, you were now in matric.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes.
Mr Sauls: And then you start at the tavern.
Mr Mogotsi: Then I went back home. That is when I went back home. I was doing correspondence classes, and I was selling, yes. At that time, I called it a tavern; it was more like a shebeen. It was not licensed. Then I rented the one which was licensed.
Mr Sauls: So, at the age of 17 to 18, between 17, going on 18, you started an unlicensed tavern?
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, I was selling.
Mr Sauls: Now, why did you, because of course, you were not an MK member, you did not, and the classes were not MK training. It was just-
Mr Mogotsi: It was my brother who got integrated and my cousin.
Mr Sauls: I see. So you started this tavern?
Mr Mogotsi: Yes.
Mr Sauls: You ran it for how long?
Mr Mogotsi: More than, almost two years. The first one was just a shebeen. Then I rented this licensed one from 1998, I believe.
Mr Sauls: So you start the tavern, and you run the tavern until when?
Mr Mogotsi: Till today. Then I opened another one.
Mr Sauls: So your core business is licensed now?
Mr Mogotsi: All of them are licensed.
Mr Sauls: So your core business is running taverns?
Mr Mogotsi: It is the liquor business, yes. I did attempt the wholesale that people were buying from me, and ever since it closed during Covid, I have never opened again.
Mr Sauls: So now in paragraph 12, you say, ‘while I was at the MK self-defence unit, I gained a lot of experience in clandestine gathering of intelligence and the practice of counterintelligence.’ You have a lot of experience. So, what did you actually do between the ages of 15 and 22 years old in 1999 that gave you a lot of experience? Experience, not training. Experience in secret intelligence gathering and counterintelligence—a lot.
Mr Mogotsi: I hear the word a lot is emphasised. To me, it was a lot, probably. That is where the word which was said here, that I became. Those years we used to be called ‘mpimpis’. I was very much involved in giving information to the police and all those things—a lot.
Mr Sauls: So your experience is in giving information.
Mr Mogotsi: I am talking about that time.
Mr Sauls: Yes, we are talking about that time. Because in paragraph 12, you say, in 1999. So now you are working back. You have a lot of experience in intelligence gathering and counterintelligence. A lot of experience.
Mr Mogotsi: You see, the moment there is a plan of a robbery and the information reaches the police, whatever action they take is to counter the intelligence of the criminal.
Mr Sauls: Yes, but you see what we are getting to now. You are claiming specialised training. And I want to understand the training that you got. Now you have a lot of experience. So, from 1993 to 1999, three of those years, you were learning. Out of those first three years, from 15 years, you had one year of a small class?
Mr Mogotsi: Yes.
Mr Sauls: Then you take that small specialised training. Then you do not practise it. You start a tavern.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes.
Mr Sauls: Then you spent all those years until 1999. You run a successful tavern. It is successful, correct?
Mr Mogotsi: Those years, yes, it was.
Mr Sauls: For a business to be successful, would you agree that it requires your focus? Your 100% focus?
Mr Mogotsi: Kind of, yes.
Mr Sauls: So, you have more experience in running a tavern?
Mr Mogotsi: Not really. Even when I was running a tavern, that is what I am saying. That is when I got very involved with the police. We travelled to so many places. In those years, there used to be a lot of armed robberies, stabbing-
Mr Sauls: But then that means the experience you are referring to in paragraph 12 is not what you want us to believe as specialised intelligence gathering and counterintelligence. Because you cannot do that and run a tavern. I put it to you that what I can agree on is that you have a lot of experience in running a tavern.
Mr Mogotsi: No.
Mr Sauls: You do not have experience in that?
Mr Mogotsi: No, I know. I know a lot about that.
Mr Sauls: Do you know why you have experience in it?
Mr Mogotsi: Because you say I spend a lot of time there.
Mr Sauls: No, I am not saying that. You say-
Mr Mogotsi: It is not that when you run a tavern, people are working for you. It is not that you-

Mr Sauls: You see, when you use the word working with the police, it does not mean what you say in paragraph 12. Because there is a specific criterion for you to be considered someone with specialised training. Number one, you did not receive specialised training.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, but you call it specialised training.
Mr Sauls: That is what it means.

Mr Sauls: So you cannot say you have experience in that?

You were part of a youth empowerment programme for young people, an after-school programme. That is what you were.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, you may call it that way.

Whether it is supported by evidence, I cannot say. Mr Mogotsi: That is how you put it.
Mr Malema: Because you were not in the SDU. If you were in the SDU, you could not respond the way you are responding. There was no SDU in 1993.
Mr Mogotsi: It was when?
Mr Malema: The SDU was formed in the late 1980s, following the violence with the state, following two states of emergency by the state. That was a violent unit of the youth responding to the violent state. That youth was armed. It is not an after-school programme. That is a distortion of the struggle of the youth of South Africa. We are former leaders of the youth who cannot sit here and allow you to say it was some 1993 programme that was an after-school programme. That was a military activity of the youth of South Africa, following two states of emergency, the formation of SAICO. The youth armed themselves, partly responding to the call by OR Tambo to make South Africa ungovernable.
Mr Mogotsi: It was 1986, and after Nelson Mandela's political campaign. But I am saying, in 1993, at our age, there was a self-defence unit. I insist we never carried guns. I also learned that is why I say, even during Nelson Mandela's campaign, I was young, and I saw it. I could have made it my story. Three people were killed in the same formation. I was not part of it. The SDU that we had in Thembelihle and other parts here, when you go to the Orange Farm side, which was still shacks at that time, that is where I participated. It is not the military one. I could not extort or change. They may have called themselves the SDU. That is why I said, when we joined, we did not know it was the SDU, or that it was going to be governed by whoever. When they came, that is when they started telling us about the whole thing.
Mr Malema: But the SDU, there is no other SDU. The SDU is directly linked with MK. When Mandela suspended the armed struggle, among other things, he suspended the armed activity of the youth of the SDU. So he is right to say this is not SDU. This is something else that is youth participation. Let it not be linked with MK, as if it is that SDU that was militarily opposed to the violent state, and was armed.
Acting Chairperson: You are claiming that you participated in the after-school youth empowerment programme.
Mr Mogotsi: No, it was not youth empowerment. I know it was called SDU. I was not the only member. Many of them are still alive. It was mostly training, even in classes. It was different. We never had arms or anything.

Mr Mogotsi: It is interrogating the evidence that is provided.
Ms Sangoni: I thought that was what the Members were doing. Let me try. You have been very concerned about being called a liar. Mr Mogotsi: These are bundles; the exhibit bundles of all communications between me-
Acting Chairperson: Maybe put it differently. These articles are shared by you with Mr Matlala. What was the intention? What did you want Mr Matlala to do with this information?
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, there is that information. Remember, I sent a lot of these articles. One piece of information, when he told me he had the IPID guy, I thought he could share with him. But where there is an article that says, I really cannot see this, what he is referring to. And I do not know if I should say do this or do not do that. But I know there was information that I gave to Mr Matlala with the hope that it would reach the IPID guy.
Acting Chairperson: So what will the IPID guy do with this stern warning issued by President Ramaphosa to Generals Masemola and Khumalo?
Mr Mogotsi: No, these other ones were articles. That is why I am saying there is a message that speaks about the properties and other things.
Acting Chairperson: No, no, no. Mr Mogotsi, you yourself sent articles through WhatsApp to Mr Matlala. So the question is, what was the intention of sending those messages? Your response may be to send them to the IPID guy. But the point is, what will the IPID guy do with an article that says President Ramaphosa sends a stern warning to Generals Masemola and Khumalo?
Mr Mogotsi: Now, as I said, remember Mr Skosana is handling so many articles, and I do not see what he is talking about. And I am saying I do not know if-
Acting Chairperson: Now, make an example of BM7.63. He started there. What you are saying is a second incident. Both the evidence leaders and Mr Skosana referred you to BM7.63, which is on page 101, where there is an article about President Ramaphosa issuing a stern warning against the head of Crime Intelligence and the National Commissioner. The question Mr Skosana is asking is, what value should be attached to this information as you send it to Mr Matlala? You are unable to answer. But you say maybe to send it to the IPID guy. The question remains, for the IPID guy to do what with this information?
Mr Mogotsi: Honestly speaking, I am sitting, I am very exhausted. And when he speaks, I am trying to check which messages he is referring to. And he said this in the book. But I am saying, if there are articles, obviously, it is for him to read. I thought he was referring to the information I spoke about earlier, about the properties and other things. He could share that with the IPID guy. But this was for him to read.
Mr Skosana: The issue, unfortunately, is that the witness wants me to ask him what he wants. He wants me to ask him about the house which was bought for R23 million, am I right? I am going to ask you about the evidence as well. You said it is registered. Is there anything wrong if it is registered in your name? You said you cannot reveal other issues. Why are you revealing this about this house? Why are you revealing that it is registered in somebody’s name?
Mr Mogotsi: No, I did not mention the name of the person.
Mr Skosana: Yes, but you said it is registered. And you said you can give us the name, am I right?
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, but I never gave the name.
Mr Skosana: Yes, now, I know you are very passionate about that house. Everyone who came, not everyone, most of you are very passionate about that house. You mentioned, unfortunately, two, I will call them comrades, Lieutenant General Mfazi. You said in your statement that he was aware that you are good at counterintelligence. Let me put it like that. How so? Because, unfortunately, he cannot speak for himself. May his soul rest in peace. But you have mentioned him, and you have mentioned Mr Mthethwa. Both comrades are no more. Now, I am asking you, in your submission here, that Lieutenant General Mfazi knew that you are good at counterintelligence. Did you convince him, or have you done something for him before?
Mr Mogotsi: No, during 2009 to a certain period that is mentioned there. Not necessarily working with him, but two people somehow spoke to him that he should bring me on board to assist in this particular project.
Mr Skosana: Who were those two people?
Mr Mogotsi: One of them is alive, is the current co-handler, and another one is no more.
Mr Mogotsi: And that is why, if you read the part that I spoke about, it is not to say General Mfazi did not know me as a person standing there. But the question you asked was, did I speak to him? I was told, no, we have to go and meet General Mfazi. And then I came, and we went to meet him with an additional person who then spoke about this issue and said this comrade can assist us in this matter. This does not require a police officer. It requires someone flexible.
Mr Skosana: So that was General Mfazi who said that to you?
Mr Mogotsi: When I was present, he did say that, yes.
Mr Skosana: So he introduced you to your co-handlers?
Mr Mogotsi: No, he introduced them because I already had a handler. When he brought the co-handler, I also knew this co-handler was the one I said was still there.
Mr Skosana: You know, I am going to make an example that the similarity between you and Mr Paul O’Sullivan is relevant. Am I right?
Mr Mogotsi: Because we are hiding most of the things you do not want to give evidence on, yes. But I have now offered, and I have requested, even though I came here seven days ago, it is now becoming very short for the information you want. If I do not pass that test, I know a perjury case will be opened. I wish, I so wish, that as I engage with the Secretary of Parliament, we could have maybe a few people, and they could get the joint Standing Committee on Intelligence. It is much easier to present the evidence that you actually require.
Mr Skosana: I just wanted to say to the witness, please, casting aspersions on King Misizulu and Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi as well. We need that evidence because it has created problems and havoc.
DA
Mr D Klopper (DA): Mr Mogotsi, it is quite strange that when you were asked earlier on who you believe. You said you believe the one who supplies proof. Mr Mogotsi, I am sure you can understand the irony in that, as you have provided very little evidence to this Committee. You describe yourself as a contact agent, Mr Mogotsi, and you state that you were privy to sensitive information regarding SAPS and Crime Intelligence. Have you, at any stage in the execution of these activities, had any contact with current sitting Members of Parliament other than Minister Mchunu, which we have already established?
Mr Mogotsi: No, no, which means in the relationship?
Mr Klopper: Did you have any communication with any current sitting Member of Parliament in exercising your duty with the information that you had?
Mr Mogotsi: No, no.
Mr Klopper: You were tasked with high-level corruption within SAPS and Crime Intelligence. In paragraph 22, you refer to engagements involving senior officials and political figures. Did you provide any information, specifically intelligence, briefing or documentation, to any sitting Member of Parliament from these investigations?
Mr Mogotsi: No.
Mr Klopper: Did you ever receive classified confidential operational information from any Member of Parliament?
Mr Mogotsi: Never, never received.
Mr Klopper: In BM2 page 5, the affidavit stating that you were not an agent, you said this was fabricated by General Khumalo and General Lushaba, who were instructed to do so. Do you have any proof of that instruction?
Mr Mogotsi: I did admit I do not have proof.
Mr Klopper: That is fine. You also defended the basis of the affidavit, saying that the person deposing the affidavit was new. I do not understand what you mean by the person is new, because surely the systems and the records they keep are not new. They have to keep some form of record of who works there. Can you explain that?
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, but as I also mentioned, the agent programme I am referring to is not where I fall under.
Mr Klopper: You have, throughout the day, claimed that the process is infiltrating Mr Matlala's networks. Yet you have spoken about legal strategies with him and solving problems. Do you realise that this is not infiltration but abatement? You were encouraging, and you were assisting in an illegal manner.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, but I have never taken any action in relation to that.
Mr Klopper: But you were sharing information with Mr Matlala, correct?
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: All payments that you received were in cash, whether as an informer or on contact agencies. Is there any independent audit trail verifying your role? How much money has been paid over the years? Will you please supply that, including all the letters and information you will supply to the Committee?
Mr Mogotsi: No, I always sign that paper and give it back.
Mr Klopper: Will that be able to be supplied to the Committee?
Mr Mogotsi: I will have to find out.
Mr Klopper: Paragraph 25, you said you obtained information. Did you personally witness any CIA recruitment? In other words, were you there or do you have any proof at your disposal, or will it be able to be provided? Because you spoke about the CIA recruitments.
Mr Mogotsi: No, I am basing my information on that particular case that was opened, and I am supposed to bring it forth.
Mr Klopper: So this is not information that you have. It is secondhand information.
Mr Mogotsi: Of the person who opened the case.
Mr Klopper: Yes.
Mr Mogotsi: I believe we will be able to obtain something about it.
Mr Klopper: I know you touched on this earlier in the verbal submissions, but to me it was not clear, and I have to go back. What direct evidence do you have that Western interests, including coal experts, were linked to Israeli interests? Is it personal knowledge, or again, is it information that was given to you?
Mr Mogotsi: Not personal information.
Mr Klopper: You, in your own words, Mr Mogotsi, used the words that they were activated in that capacity to protect Western interests. King Misizulu, during this time, Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi was tasked with neutralising any opposition. These are your words. You say it as a matter of fact in the statement.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, as I have received information from-
Mr Klopper: As you received it. But in the statement, as it stands here, word by word, it is your version, your fact. So you are making this as a matter of fact, using those words in this allegation. I will move on. Paragraph 28, you say that there were Western interests and that the South African government may hold the coal exports. Yet if you go and actually look at the coal exports, it was 47.2 million tonnes in ‘23, 56 million in ‘24, and 57 million in ‘25. So, Mr Mogotsi, if there was an imminent threat from the Western coal interest in Richards Bay, how could an experienced contact agent like yourself not see that the export coal actually was increasing? There was no threat.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, I never said it stopped. I said that there was a fear that it may be stopped due to the case in the ICJ and this altercation between the brothers. As I said, it is my duty. I am realising the veracity, the weight this is carrying, and how it will affect my life. I will make sure that I obtain information and, where I am wrong, I will definitely retract openly and in public.
Mr Klopper: I think I have to stop you, Mr Mogotsi. Did you have any other reasons for your presence in Richards Bay other than that?
Mr Mogotsi: No, I do not.
Mr Klopper: Mr Mogotsi, in paragraph 35, you used the words "my suspicion". Paragraph 36, "confirm the possibility". In paragraph 37, "suspicion". This was all referring to the CIA agent's allegations that you made. So throughout these paragraphs, you are alluding to Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi being a CIA agent. You refer to suspicion and possibility. Are you therefore saying there is no confirmed proof of CIA recruitment in your affidavit? Because in your own words, you say you suspect, you think, you possibly. So is he or isn't he?
Mr Mogotsi: He isn't.
Mr Klopper: So there is no proof? Because using these words signals to us that you are not even sure. Otherwise, you would say yes, he is, and that is it.
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: What information did you receive in Kenya?
Mr Malema: What is correct? Are we now at one that Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi is not a CIA agent?
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct. He is saying to me, as I sit now-
Mr Malema: You must say it.
Mr Mogotsi: I did not provide any tangible evidence. I kept on saying I would bring information that must be corroborated. That is why I am requesting that I get this information and make sure it gets corroborated.
Mr Malema: No, but you just-
Mr Mogotsi: At the moment, as we speak, it is correct.
Mr Malema: No, no, you do not say it is correct. You must say it. You must say Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi is not a CIA agent.
Mr Mogotsi: He is not until I provide-
Mr Malema: No, no, you cannot-
Mr Mogotsi: He is not a CIA agent.
Mr Malema: Who is not?
Mr Mogotsi: Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi.
Mr Malema: He is not what?
Mr Mogotsi: A CIA agent.
Ms Sangoni: I may be covered by Mr Malema, but maybe to take it one step further, Mr Mogotsi, are you now withdrawing the allegations that you have made against both Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi and King Misizulu being CIA agents?
Mr Mogotsi: No.
Ms Sangoni: You have just said he is not. Can you withdraw the allegations that you have made?
Mr Mogotsi: Members, if I have, for me to say he is not is because at the moment there is no proof that I am able to provide. Hence, I have said I am requesting only time.
Ms Sangoni: You said he who alleges must prove, in which case they must not even make the allegation. Kindly withdraw the allegations. If you think you will get proof, you can raise them again, but do not put things before us that you are not able to prove. Those are your words. He who alleges must prove.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, based on the statement, because there is no statement, I hereby withdraw.
Acting Chairperson: We are actually giving you one minute to apologise to both Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi and King Msizulu officially.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, as I do. I retract the statement regarding both Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi and King Misizulu.
Acting Chairperson: We are giving you 30 seconds to officially apologise for having cast aspersions on both Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi and King Misizulu of AmaZulu.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, I hereby apologise for casting aspersions on Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi and King Misizulu, the Majesty.
Mr Klopper: When you were in Kenya, what evidence did you receive there? Can you communicate it, or can't you?
Mr Mogotsi: Nothing was confirmed.
Mr Klopper: So the only person you allude to having informed about what transpired there was former Minister Mthethwa, who has passed away. Yet today you spoke about your handler in every single case. But in this case specifically, it had to be Minister Mthethwa. Why the difference?
Mr Mogotsi: I also told the handler. I told him.
Mr Klopper: But why did you need to tell Minister Mthethwa as well?
Mr Mogotsi: He was the one who said I must go. I can get something from Kenya.
Mr Klopper: You made another allegation against Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi. You wrote specifically the words on numerous occasions: send Members of PKTT to intimidate, harass, and attempt murder. This was covered slightly. How many attempted murders do you allege were harassments or intimidations? Also, the names, dates, and case numbers. This can also be sent to the Committee.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes. Based on that case number, it was the harassment, and the case that they alleged was open. The case number proves their statement.
Mr Klopper: Is it only one case?
Mr Mogotsi: We have them with one case. Therefore, it means all these other cases were mentioned in the statement of the case that was open.
Mr Klopper: What exactly did you mean when you said it would solve all his difficulties when speaking to Mr Matlala, meeting somebody? What did he mean specifically by would solve all his difficulties? Do you know?
Mr Mogotsi: I did not know.
Mr Klopper: You described tracking at the airport, following vehicles, and staking out residents, first, from an Uber driver. Mr Mogotsi was an Uber driver, also an informant or agent?
Mr Mogotsi: No, he was not.
Mr Klopper: Did you pay him?
Mr Mogotsi: I just thanked him.
Mr Klopper: You didn't pay him.
Mr Mogotsi: No, I thanked him with something.
Mr Klopper: What was that something?
Mr Mogotsi: R500.
Mr Klopper: What is the Uber driver's name?
Mr Mogotsi: I don't. Skhalo?
Mr Klopper: Do you have his contact details?
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, I had his contact details.
Mr Klopper: Do you have them?
Mr Mogotsi: No, I do not have them.
Mr Klopper: So there is no credibility in that statement either. Under what statutory authority were you conducting this surveillance?
Mr Mogotsi: I did not understand the question.
Mr Klopper: Under what statutory authority were you conducting this surveillance of following, tracking his car, and staking out the residents?
Mr Mogotsi: I am not familiar or conversant with statutory authorities.
Mr Klopper: Can you produce any written task order to actually confirm that you had the authority to do what you were doing?
Mr Mogotsi: I only spoke to my handler and followed him.
Mr Klopper: But this handler is your cover for every question that we ask you, Mr Mogotsi. Anything that you cannot answer, you blame the handler.
Mr Mogotsi: No, I am not blaming him.
Mr Klopper: So, when something transpires and goes sour, what happens then, because your handler has not come to your rescue yet?
Mr Mogotsi: I do not know what you mean by sour.
Mr Klopper: I am saying if something went sour, you end up in jail for doing something. You could be considered a stalker if you do not have a formal request. You are following someone without authority, then you are having Uber drivers follow them and not just them, their wife as well, which is in your affidavit.
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: So you are actually saying I am correct. You could be a stalker.
Mr Mogotsi: If it is interpreted the way you say it, it is more like a stalker.
Mr Klopper: Did you witness any money being handed over from Mr Matlala to Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi?
Mr Mogotsi: No, he only said that.
Mr Klopper: So this allegation is entirely based on what Mr Matlala told you.
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: Did you ask what it was for?
Mr Mogotsi: No, he was calling me from prison. He just spoke to me, and the phone went off.
Mr Klopper: You were investigating Mr Matlala. Did you not think that this information would have been vital?
Mr Mogotsi: No, he was in prison, and they took the phone from him when he told me I was with that man, and I gave him something.
Mr Klopper: You deliberately created a false perception in the mind of Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi in your words to gain his trust. Is this correct?
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: You misinterpreted access to the minister's office, correct? You created an impression.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes.
Mr Klopper: You attached official documents to give artificial credibility to your communications.
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: You said you attached them to the affidavit, paragraph 50.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, that is correct.
Mr Klopper: 51 and 124. You lied to create a false impression about being with Minister Mchunu in Lusikisiki.
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: You admit to leaking disciplinary information to the media, correct?
Mr Mogotsi: In which matter?
Mr Klopper: The General Khan disciplinary.
Mr Mogotsi: That is correct.
Mr Klopper: You initiated a section 252A operation with the Hawks to catch perpetrators, yet you acknowledge you leaked to the media.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, but I leaked the information-
Mr Klopper: Just a quick yes or no. I am running out of time.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, the message was leaked as to what was going to happen during the hearing.
Mr Klopper: Mr Mogotsi, you have really dressed up the word lying in very fancy clothes. You use words like you created false perceptions in paragraph 42. You misrepresented your access to the minister in paragraph 46. You lied about Lusikisiki, which you say you did. You admit to leaking information about proceedings, which I think were internal. It is you who has painted yourself a professional liar, Mr Mogotsi—no one else. You relied on suspicion and the possibility of the CIA claims. You have, through your verbal testimony, accepted that you took money from someone that you were investigating, namely Mr Matlala. Mr Malema here even said to you, it is extortion. You were charged so many times you cannot even remember for what anymore, including fraud, motor theft, and assault. Verbally, when you were asked, “ Did you murder someone, you said yes, and then you proceeded to tell us it is private. Given these admissions, why should this Committee accept any of your allegations of attempted murders or CIA? To that effect, how can the Committee believe anything you say? Anything you say might be a construction of perception you yourself wish to create. Mr Mogotsi, these are your words as you described them today.
Final Questions from Evidence Leaders
Acting Chairperson: Evidence leaders, will you have something to deal with?
Adv Mokhoaetsi: We are fine. I think we have ventilated all issues.
Final Questions from Members
Mr Skosana: I want to go back. If you remember when I asked you, I said page 25. I said to you, “ Do you have any evidence to support the allegations against Lieutenant General Mkhwanazi and King Misizulu that they are CIA agents? If not, it is perjury. Now, because you have confirmed when Mr Malema and Ms Sangoni asked you about this matter, it means it is perjury.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, and when I responded to you, I requested that, based on the statement that I have seen that was written, making this allegation, and she said no, because you do not have a statement and you are promising to make any submission. Until then, there is no such thing. That is what I admitted, and I do admit.
Mr Skosana: Yes, but you agree that you have withdrawn that now.
Mr Mogotsi: Yes, I have withdrawn-
Mr Skosana: So the perjury for what I said to you stands. I just wanted to remind the witness of that.
Mr Mogotsi: I want to thank the Committee. Now it means that on my submissions, I have little to submit. I was warned I must be honest-
Acting Chairperson: Who warned you?
Mr Mogotsi: A very high senior person. When you get there, go and retract immediately. And I said, and I was boosting, calling the person who wrote the statement, are you still willing to testify? He said yes. Hey, I withdraw. I do not even want the statement. Thank you, Chair.
Acting Chairperson’s Closing Remarks
Acting Chairperson: Thank you so much, Mr Brown Mogotsi. Members, thank you so much. I think from the aspects of the evidence of the weakness you covered, I will not be asking any questions because I think you have satisfactorily covered all aspects that the witness would have raised. Of course, evidence leaders will help us and guide us with our secretariat on writing to the Crime Intelligence boss on several issues that would have been raised by Members that need the institution's confirmation in respect of Mr Brown Mogotsi. Mr Mogotsi, thank you so much for coming to Parliament. We may have had some minor hurdles in terms of securing your appearance at the time we wanted, but as you have committed to coming, we just need to fix a few minor logistical issues. We do not doubt that your evidence, including the parts that have been retracted, is valuable in arriving at findings and recommendations of what needs to be done. What joins us together is that this Committee comprises several political parties. Still, we speak in unison solely because justice must be done for the people of South Africa. We want to rid state institutions of any possible wrongdoing that they might be engulfed in. That is why you see us united as a multi-party Committee. We thank you so much. We wish you safety. We may need you for other purposes in the near future. The work of Parliament continues. Our term ends in 2029, so we are still here for quite some time. There may be other obligations that may need you. We wish you safe travel back home and that you remain safe whilst state institutions are still dealing with these matters. Members meeting on Thursday, now not tomorrow, and we have changed this to 11:00. We are moving earlier to 10:00 so that as we approach the end, we can finish a lot of things. The evidence leaders have briefed us that Mr Paul O'Sullivan is coming back to finish his testimony. General Kgomotso Phahlane is also coming on the same Thursday to finish his testimony. Bring some water and sweets for the day. It is going to be busy. Thank you so much, Members, staff, and the media.
The meeting is adjourned.
